<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Similar to primes, use sieve up to LIMIT=200000 to identify primes.
# Step 2: Collect composites as numbers >1 that are not prime.
# Step 3: The first 10000 composites will be well within LIMIT (density of composites is high).
# Step 4: Read all input at once for speed.
# Step 5: For each n, output first n composites.

import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

LIMIT = 200000
is_prime = [True] * (LIMIT + 1)
is_prime[0] = is_prime[1] = False
for i in range(2, int(LIMIT**0.5) + 1):
    if is_prime[i]:
        for j in range(i*i, LIMIT + 1, i):
            is_prime[j] = False

composites = [i for i in range(1, LIMIT + 1) if i > 1 and not is_prime[i]]

outputs = []
for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    first_n_composites = composites[:n]
    outputs.append(' '.join(map(str, first_n_composites)))

print('\n'.join(outputs))

<description>
Problem: Given the number of test cases t (1 ≤ t ≤ 100), followed by t lines each containing an integer n (1 ≤ n ≤ 1000), with the sum of n over all test cases ≤ 10^4, for each test case, output the first n perfect squares (1^2, 2^2, ..., n^2) in increasing order. They satisfy 1 ≤ a_i ≤ 10^9 since 1000^2 = 10^6 < 10^9.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: No precomputation needed; perfect squares are i*i for i=1 to n.
# Step 2: Read t, then for each test case read n.
# Step 3: Generate [1*1, 2*2, ..., n*n] and print joined by space.
# Step 4: Use fast input for consistency.
# Step 5: Since n<=1000, t<=100, very efficient.

import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

outputs = []
for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    squares = [str(i * i) for i in range(1, n + 1)]
    outputs.append(' '.join(squares))

print('\n'.join(outputs))
</code>
<planning>

# 4. Batch outputs for single print.
# 5. Constraints ensure no overflow (1000^2=1e6 <<1e9).

<algorithm>
# Identify the algorithm (Brute-force, Dynamic Programming, Divide-and-conquer, Greedy, Backtracking, Recursive, Binary search, and so on) that needs to be used to solve the original problem.
Greedy

# Write a useful tutorial about the above mentioned algorithms. Provide a high level generic tutorial for solving this types of problem. Do not generate code.
Greedy Algorithm Tutorial for Constructing Sequences with Local Constraints:

The original problem involves constructing a strictly increasing sequence where each element satisfies a local constraint with its immediate predecessor (a_i not divisible by a_{i-1}). This is a classic case for the Greedy algorithm, where we make the locally optimal choice at each step that satisfies the constraints, hoping (and proving) it leads to a global solution.

High-level Generic Steps for Greedy Construction in Similar Problems:
1. **Understand Constraints**: Identify local (adjacent/pairwise) and global (e.g., range, monotonicity) conditions. Here, strictly increasing (a_i > a_{i-1}), 1 ≤ a_i ≤ 10^9, and a_i % a_{i-1} ≠ 0.
2. **Initialize**: Choose a starting value a_1 that works (e.g., smallest possible like 1 for n=1, or 2 for larger n).
3. **Greedy Choice**: For each next position i=2 to n, select the smallest (or any feasible) a_i > a_{i-1} that satisfies the constraint with a_{i-1}. This keeps numbers small and within bounds.
   - In this problem, a_i = a_{i-1} + 1 always works because (a_{i-1} + 1) % a_{i-1} = 1 ≠ 0, and it's the smallest possible increase.
4. **Verify Feasibility**: Ensure the choice doesn't violate future possibilities. Here, since the modulus condition is independent and the range is huge (10^9), consecutive integers work indefinitely.
5. **Handle Edge Cases**: n=1 (trivial), n=2 (e.g., 2 3).
6. **Efficiency**: O(n) time per test case, perfect for n≤1000, sum n≤10^4.
7. **Proof of Correctness**: Show existence (given), and that greedy doesn't get stuck (consecutive always satisfies).
8. **Alternatives if Greedy Fails**: If local optimum traps (rare here), consider backtracking or DP, but greedy shines for "matroid-like" or chain constraints.

Common Pitfalls: Starting with 1 for n>1 fails (everything divisible by 1). Solution: Start from 2.
Extensions: For stricter constraints (e.g., no divisibility pairwise), use primes or odds; for global opts, adjust greedy (e.g., largest possible).
This approach scales to problems like scheduling, coin change (canonical greedy), or sequence building with adjacent inequalities.
</algorithm>

<description>
# Problem: Large Number String Comparison
You are given multiple test cases. For each test case, two strings A and B are provided, each representing a positive integer without leading zeros (length of each string between 1 and 10^5). Compare A and B as numbers and output '&lt;' if A &lt; B, '&gt;' if A &gt; B, '=' if equal.
Input:
First line: integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^4)
Next 2*t lines: for each test case, one line for A, one line for B.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Import necessary modules for efficient input reading since strings can be long and t up to 1e4.
import sys

# Step 2: Read all input at once for efficiency.
data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
idx = 0

# Step 3: Read number of test cases.
t = int(data[idx])
idx += 1

# Step 4: Loop over each test case.
for _ in range(t):
    # Step 5: Read the two strings A and B.
    A = data[idx].strip()
    idx += 1
    B = data[idx].strip()
    idx += 1
    
    # Step 6: First, compare lengths. Longer string represents larger number (no leading zeros).
    lenA = len(A)
    lenB = len(B)
    if lenA > lenB:
        print('>')
    elif lenA < lenB:
        print('<')
    else:
        # Step 7: If lengths equal, compare strings lexicographically (valid since no leading zeros).
        if A > B:
            print('>')
        elif A < B:
            print('<')
        else:
            print('=')
</code>
<planning>
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Recognize that for positive integers without leading zeros, the number with more digits is always larger.
2. Compute lengths of A and B.
3. If lengths differ, decide based on which is longer.
4. If lengths same, since no leading zeros, direct string comparison gives the numerical order.
5. Use fast input reading to handle large input sizes (t=1e4, len=1e5 possible, though total input size large but Python handles).
6. Output the symbol for each test case immediately.
Time: O(sum lengths) = O(1e4 * 1e5) worst, but practical.
</planning>

# Problem: Version Number Comparison
You are given multiple test cases. For each test case, two version strings V1 and V2 (e.g., "1.2.3", "10.1.0") are provided. Compare the versions: split by '.', interpret parts as integers, pad shorter with zeros. Output '&lt;' if V1 &lt; V2, '&gt;' if V1 &gt; V2, '=' if equal.
Input:
First line: integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^4)
Next 2*t lines: for each test case, one line for V1, one line for V2. Each part 1-10 digits, up to 100 parts.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Import sys for efficient input.
import sys

# Step 2: Read all input.
data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
idx = 0

# Step 3: Read t.
t = int(data[idx])
idx += 1

# Step 4: For each test case.
for _ in range(t):
    V1 = data[idx].strip()
    idx += 1
    V2 = data[idx].strip()
    idx += 1
    
    # Step 5: Split into lists of integers.
    parts1 = [int(part) for part in V1.split('.')]
    parts2 = [int(part) for part in V2.split('.')]
    
    # Step 6: Get max length.
    max_len = max(len(parts1), len(parts2))
    
    # Step 7: Compare part by part, padding with 0.
    for i in range(max_len):
        n1 = parts1[i] if i < len(parts1) else 0
        n2 = parts2[i] if i < len(parts2) else 0
        if n1 > n2:
            print('>')
            break
        elif n1 < n2:
            print('<')
            break
    else:
        # Step 8: If all equal.
        print('=')
</code>

4. At first differing position, decide the order.
5. If no difference, equal.
6. Efficient for constraints: parts up to 100, t=1e4, O(100*1e4)=fine.
7. Handle input efficiently with readlines.

# Problem: Power Comparison
You are given multiple test cases. For each, four integers a, b, c, d (2 ≤ a,c ≤ 100, 1 ≤ b,d ≤ 10^9). Compare a^b and c^d. Output '&lt;' if a^b &lt; c^d, '&gt;' if greater, '=' if equal.
Input:
First line: t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^4)
Next t lines: a b c d
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Import math for log10.
import sys
import math

# Step 2: Read input.
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

# Step 3: Read t.
t = int(data[0])
ptr = 1

# Step 4: For each test case.
for _ in range(t):
    a = int(data[ptr])
    b = int(data[ptr+1])
    c = int(data[ptr+2])
    d = int(data[ptr+3])
    ptr += 4
    
    # Step 5: Compute log10(a^b) = b * log10(a)
    if b == 0:  # Though b>=1, handle
        log1 = 0.0
    else:
        log1 = b * math.log10(a)
    if d == 0:
        log2 = 0.0
    else:
        log2 = d * math.log10(c)
    
    # Step 6: Compare with small epsilon for fp precision.
    EPS = 1e-10
    if log1 > log2 + EPS:
        print('>')
    elif log1 + EPS < log2:
        print('<')
    else:
        print('=')
</code>
<planning>

3. Use log10 for convenience.
4. Account for floating-point precision with epsilon (safe for given constraints as a,c small).
5. For equality, rare but handled by epsilon.
6. Fast input parsing.
7. Alternative: compare digit counts first (floor(log)+1), then fractional parts for mantissa if equal digits.
Time: O(1) per test.
</planning>

<algorithm>
# Identify the algorithm: Ad-hoc mathematical comparison (length-based digit count comparison with exponent normalization via integer scaling).

# Tutorial: High-level generic tutorial for solving problems involving comparison of very large numbers in compact form (e.g., x * 10^p where x is small but p is large).
To solve comparisons of numbers too large to store directly (like x * 10^p with p up to 10^6), avoid constructing the full number or using floating-point logs (which risk precision errors). Instead:

1. Compute the "effective length" (number of decimal digits) for each number:
   - digits(x) = floor(log10(x)) + 1 (or len(str(x)) since x ≤ 10^6).
   - L = digits(x) + p.

2. Compare lengths L1 and L2:
   - If L1 > L2, first number > second.
   - If L1 < L2, first < second.
   - This handles ~99% of cases efficiently.

3. If L1 == L2 (rare, same order of magnitude):
   - Let d1 = digits(x1), d2 = digits(x2). Then p1 = L - d1, p2 = L - d2.
   - Normalize exponents: compare x1 * 10^{p1} ? x2 * 10^{p2} becomes x1 ? x2 * 10^{d1 - d2} (if d1 ≥ d2) or symmetric.
   - Since |d1 - d2| ≤ 6 (x ≤ 10^6), 10^{|d1-d2|} ≤ 10^6, x2 * 10^{diff} ≤ 10^{12}, fits in 64-bit integers.
   - If d1 == d2, simply x1 ? x2.
   - Adjust comparison direction based on which d is larger.

4. Edge cases:
   - x ≥ 1, no zeros.
   - Equality when scaled values equal.

This is O(1) per test (str(x) or log10 fast), precise (no FP issues), works for t=10^4.
Generic for similar: factorial sizes (Stirling), powers (but scale carefully), scientific notation.
</algorithm>
</root>
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Use fast input reading (e.g., sys.stdin.read() in Python) to process all test cases efficiently given t ≤ 10^4.
2. For each test case, parse x1, p1, x2, p2.
3. Compute digit lengths: d1 = len(str(x1)), d2 = len(str(x2)) (O(1) per test).
4. Compute effective lengths: L1 = d1 + p1, L2 = d2 + p2.
5. If L1 > L2, output '>'; if L1 < L2, output '<'.
6. If L1 == L2:
   - If d1 == d2, compare x1 and x2 directly.
   - If d1 > d2, compute scaled = x2 * (10 ** (d1 - d2)); compare x1 ? scaled (if x1 > scaled: '>', x1 < scaled: '<', else '=').
   - If d2 > d1, compute scaled = x1 * (10 ** (d2 - d1)); compare scaled ? x2 (if scaled > x2: '>', scaled < x2: '<', else '=').
7. Output the result for each test case immediately.
Time: O(t) = O(10^4), since per-test operations are O(1) (max exponent diff ≤ 6, fits in 64-bit integers). Precise, no floating-point issues. Handles all edges (p=0, x=1, equal cases via scaling).

When lengths are equal, it correctly scales the smaller-digit number by multiplying by 10 raised to the digit difference (at most 6, fitting in integers without overflow in Python), and compares appropriately. This avoids direct computation of huge numbers (up to 10^(10^6+7)). All cases are handled: different lengths, same length with same/different digit counts, equality via scaling, edges like p=0, x=1, and examples match. Fast input ensures efficiency for t=10^4. No floating-point or precision issues. Time is O(t), correct and optimal.</explanation>

2. Read t (number of test cases).
3. For each test case:
   a. Read x1, p1 on first line; x2, p2 on second line.
   b. Compute d1 = to_string(x1).size() (digits of x1).
   c. Compute d2 = to_string(x2).size() (digits of x2).
   d. Compute L1 = d1 + p1; L2 = d2 + p2 (use long long for safety).
   e. If L1 > L2, output '>'; if L1 < L2, output '<'.
   f. Else (L1 == L2):
      - If d1 == d2: compare x1 ? x2 directly.
      - Else if d1 > d2: diff = d1 - d2; scaled = x2 * pow10[diff]; compare x1 ? scaled.
      - Else (d2 > d1): diff = d2 - d1; scaled = x1 * pow10[diff]; compare scaled ? x2.
      - Output '>', '<', or '=' based on comparison.
4. Use fast I/O: ios::sync_with_stdio(false); cin.tie(NULL); cout.tie(NULL).
5. Efficient: O(1) per test (to_string O(1) for x <= 10^6), total O(t) fine for t=10^4.
6. Handles edges: x=1 (d=1), x=10^6 (d=7), p=0/10^6, equal cases via exact scaling (fits long long <= 10^12).

```python
import sys

data = sys.stdin.read().split()
idx = 0
t = int(data[idx])
idx += 1
for _ in range(t):
    x1 = int(data[idx])
    p1 = int(data[idx + 1])
    idx += 2
    x2 = int(data[idx])
    p2 = int(data[idx + 1])
    idx += 2
    d1 = len(str(x1))
    d2 = len(str(x2))
    L1 = d1 + p1
    L2 = d2 + p2
    if L1 > L2:
        print('>')
    elif L1 < L2:
        print('<')
    else:
        if d1 == d2:
            if x1 > x2:
                print('>')
            elif x1 < x2:
                print('<')
            else:
                print('=')
        elif d1 > d2:
            diff = d1 - d2
            scaled = x2 * (10 ** diff)
            if x1 > scaled:
                print('>')
            elif x1 < scaled:
                print('<')
            else:
                print('=')
        else:
            diff = d2 - d1
            scaled = x1 * (10 ** diff)
            if scaled > x2:
                print('>')
            elif scaled < x2:
                print('<')
            else:
                print('=')
```

<problem>
<description>
# Exemplar Problem 1: Minimum Possible Maximum Tower Height

There are n block towers in a row, where tower i has a height of a_i. You can perform operations: choose two indices i and j (i ≠ j), move a block from tower i to tower j (decrease a_i by 1, increase a_j by 1). Any number of operations. What is the minimum possible height of the tallest tower you can achieve?

Input:
First line: t (1 ≤ t ≤ 1000)
Per test case: n (2 ≤ n ≤ 100), then n integers a_i (1 ≤ a_i ≤ 10^7)

Output:
For each test case, one integer: the minimum possible max height.

Example:
Input:
3
3
10 10 10
4
3 2 1 2
5
1 2 3 1 5
Output:
10
2
3
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

# Step 1: Understand that we can freely redistribute blocks, total sum S is fixed.
# The min max height is ceil(S / n), which is (S + n - 1) // n

# Step 2: Use fast input reading since t=1000, n=100.
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

# Step 3: Parse input.
index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

# Step 4: Process each test case.
for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    a = [int(data[index + i]) for i in range(n)]
    index += n
    S = sum(a)
    # Step 5: Compute ceil(S / n)
    min_max = (S + n - 1) // n
    print(min_max)
</code>

<description>
# Exemplar Problem 3: Minimum Operations to Achieve Even Distribution

There are n block towers in a row, where tower i has a height of a_i. You can perform operations: choose two indices i and j (i ≠ j), move a block from tower i to tower j. Compute the minimum number of operations to make all heights either floor(S/n) or ceil(S/n), with correct counts.

Input:
First line: t (1 ≤ t ≤ 1000)
Per test case: n (2 ≤ n ≤ 100), then n integers a_i (1 ≤ a_i ≤ 10^7)

Output:
For each test case, one integer: the min operations.

Example:
Input:
3
3
10 10 10
4
3 2 1 2
5
1 2 3 1 5
Output:
0
1
2
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

# Step 1: Even distribution: base = S//n, r = S%n, targets: (n-r) at base, r at base+1.
# Step 2: To min moves (each move transfers 1 block), compute min total blocks moved = (1/2) * min sum |a_i - t_pi(i)|
# Step 3: Min sum abs diff with fixed multiset t: sort a and sort t, pair in order.
# Step 4: Since n small, easy.

import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    a = [int(data[index + i]) for i in range(n)]
    index += n
    S = sum(a)
    base = S // n
    r = S % n
    a.sort()
    # Create sorted targets
    targets = [base] * (n - r) + [base + 1] * r
    # Compute sum abs diff
    total_diff = 0
    for i in range(n):
        total_diff += abs(a[i] - targets[i])
    # Min moves = total_diff // 2
    print(total_diff // 2)
</code>
<planning>
# Planning to solve this problem.
- Compute S, base = S//n, r = S%n.
- Target multiset: base (n-r times), base+1 (r times).
- Sort the current heights a.
- Sort targets (already sorted).
- Compute sum_{i=0}^{n-1} |a[i] - targets[i]| ; this is minimal L1 distance.
- Since each operation reduces total diff by 2 (one +1, one -1), min ops = that sum // 2.
- Implement efficiently with sorting (n log n fine).
</planning>
</problem>
<algorithm>
# The algorithm that needs to be used to solve the original problem is Greedy (even distribution of resources).

# High-level generic tutorial for solving this type of problem (minimizing max-min difference via redistribution operations):

## Key Insight:
Problems involving minimizing the range (max - min) of an array by transferring units between elements (unlimited operations, any pair) boil down to redistributing the total sum S as evenly as possible across n elements. The optimal configuration has all values at floor(S/n) or ceil(S/n).

## Steps to Solve:
1. **Compute total sum S**: This is invariant under operations.
2. **Calculate base values**:
   - Let `base = S // n`
   - Let `remainder = S % n`
   - Optimal heights: `remainder` towers at `base + 1`, `n - remainder` at `base`.
3. **Determine min ugliness**:
   - If `remainder == 0`, all equal to `base`, ugliness = 0.
   - Else, heights `base` and `base + 1`, ugliness = 1.
4. **Verify feasibility**: Since operations allow arbitrary transfers (complete graph), any distribution with sum S and non-negative integers is achievable (here, guaranteed >=0).

## Why Greedy?
- Greedily assigning extras to make heights differ by at most 1 minimizes the range.
- No better range possible: pigeonhole principle forces some at floor/ceil if S not divisible by n.

## Edge Cases:
- All equal initially: 0.
- S % n == 0: 0.
- Otherwise: 1.
- Constraints ensure base >=1, but works generally.

## Time Complexity: O(n) per test (sum), but insight makes it O(1) post-sum.

## Generalization:
- If adjacent-only moves: Use two pointers or prefix sums for min moves, but range still 0/1.
- With limits on operations: Binary search on range D, check feasibility via greedy prefix.
- For min moves to even dist: Sort + pair with targets (as in exemplar 3).
</algorithm>
</root>

- Output 0 if S % n == 0 else 1.
- Use 64-bit integers for sum (e.g., long long in C++, int in Python).
- Read t, then for each test: read n, read n integers and sum.

```xml
<problem>
<description>
Problem 1: Given a string s consisting of lowercase English letters, rearrange its characters to form the lexicographically smallest string possible such that no two identical characters are adjacent. If it is impossible, return an empty string "".
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

# Step 1: Count the frequency of each character using Counter.
from collections import Counter

# Step 2: We will build the result string position by position. At each position, try the smallest possible character
# that has remaining frequency > 0 and is not the same as the last character in the current result.
# Since there are only 26 letters, iterating over sorted keys is efficient.

# Step 3: If at any point we cannot find such a character, it's impossible.

# Step 4: Decrement the frequency after placing a character.

def smallest_rearrangement_no_adjacent(s: str) -> str:
    if not s:
        return ""
    count = Counter(s)
    result = []
    last = None
    for _ in range(len(s)):
        found = False
        for c in sorted(count.keys()):
            if count[c] > 0 and c != last:
                result.append(c)
                count[c] -= 1
                last = c
                found = True
                break
        if not found:
            return ""
    return ''.join(result)

# Example usage:
# print(smallest_rearrangement_no_adjacent("aa"))  # ""
# print(smallest_rearrangement_no_adjacent("aabb"))  # "abab"
</code>
<planning>
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Compute frequency count of all characters.
2. Initialize empty result list and no last character.
3. For each of the n positions:
   a. Iterate over characters in alphabetical order (smallest first).
   b. Pick the first (smallest) character with positive count that != last.
   c. Append to result, decrement count, update last.
   d. If none found, impossible.
4. Join result as string.
This ensures lex smallest because we always greedily pick smallest possible at each step without violating local constraint.
Verification: since adjacent constraint is local, this greedy works and produces the global lex smallest if possible.
</planning>
</problem>
<problem>
<description>
Problem 2: Given a string s, remove some duplicate letters so that every letter appears once and only once in the result. The relative order of characters must be preserved as in s. Among all possible results, return the lexicographically smallest one. (LeetCode 316: Remove Duplicate Letters)
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

# Step 1: Compute the last occurrence index of each character for greedy decision.

# Step 2: Use a stack to build the result. For each character:
# - Skip if already in stack (seen).
# - While stack top is larger than current, and stack top's last occurrence is after current position,
#   pop it (remove from seen).
# - Push current and add to seen.

# Step 3: This greedy ensures smallest lex by removing larger chars when possible later.

def removeDuplicateLetters(s: str) -> str:
    last_occurrence = {c: i for i, c in enumerate(s)}
    stack = []
    seen = set()
    for i, c in enumerate(s):
        if c in seen:
            continue
        while stack and stack[-1] > c and last_occurrence[stack[-1]] > i:
            seen.remove(stack.pop())
        stack.append(c)
        seen.add(c)
    return ''.join(stack)

# Example usage:
# print(removeDuplicateLetters("bcabc"))  # "abc"
# print(removeDuplicateLetters("cbacdcbc"))  # "acdb"
</code>
<planning>

3. Iterate through each char in s:
   a. If already seen, skip.
   b. While stack not empty, top > current char, and top's last occ > current index: pop top, remove from seen.
   c. Push current to stack, add to seen.
4. Stack is the result.
This greedy stack simulates choosing smallest possible prefix while ensuring all chars can be included later.
</planning>

<problem>
<description>
Problem 3: Given a string s and a list of pairs pairs where each pair [i, j] means you can swap s[i] and s[j] any number of times. Return the lexicographically smallest string you can obtain by performing any number of swaps. (LeetCode 1202: Smallest String With Swaps)
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

# Step 1: Use Union-Find to group indices that are connected via swaps (same component).

# Step 2: For each connected component, collect the positions and the characters at those positions.

# Step 3: Sort the positions and sort the characters in the component, then assign the smallest chars to the smallest positions.

# Step 4: Build the result string.

from typing import List
from collections import defaultdict

def smallestStringWithSwaps(s: str, pairs: List[List[int]]) -> str:
    n = len(s)
    parent = list(range(n))
    
    def find(x: int) -> int:
        if parent[x] != x:
            parent[x] = find(parent[x])
        return parent[x]
    
    def union(x: int, y: int):
        px = find(x)
        py = find(y)
        if px != py:
            parent[px] = py
    
    for i, j in pairs:
        union(i, j)
    
    groups = defaultdict(list)
    for i in range(n):
        groups[find(i)].append(i)
    
    res = list(s)
    for idxs in groups.values():
        chars = sorted(res[i] for i in idxs)
        idxs.sort()
        for pos, char in zip(idxs, chars):
            res[pos] = char
    
    return ''.join(res)

# Example usage:
# print(smallestStringWithSwaps("dcab", [[0,3],[1,2],[0,2]]))  # "abcd"
</code>
<planning>
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Initialize Union-Find for n positions.
2. For each swap pair, union the indices.
3. Group positions by their root parent (connected components).
4. For each group:
   a. Collect and sort the characters in those positions (smallest first).
   b. Sort the positions in the group (earliest first).
   c. Assign sorted chars to sorted positions.
5. Construct result from modified list.
This greedy assignment ensures smallest chars go to earliest possible positions within swap constraints.
</planning>
</problem>
<algorithm>
# Identify the algorithm: Greedy

# Write a useful tutorial about the above mentioned algorithms. Provide a high level generic tutorial for solving this types of problem.

## Tutorial: Solving Lexicographically Smallest Permutations with Constraints using Greedy

Problems requiring the lexicographically smallest permutation (rearrangement) of a string under certain constraints (e.g., no forbidden subsequences, no adjacent repeats, swap limits) are commonly solved with a **greedy** approach. The key idea is to build the string position-by-position, always choosing the smallest possible character at the current position that satisfies the constraint and guarantees a valid completion for the remaining characters.

### High-Level Steps:
1. **Precompute Frequencies**: Count occurrences of each character (use array or Counter for O(1) access).

2. **Maintain Feasibility State**: Track minimal state representing the prefix's constraint satisfaction (e.g., boolean flags for avoidance modes, counts of critical characters, stack for monotonicity, Union-Find for connectivity). This state must be updatable in O(1) or O(log n) time.

3. **Greedy Choice per Position**:
   - Iterate candidate characters in alphabetical order ('a' to 'z').
   - For each candidate with remaining count > 0:
     - Temporarily "add" it: update the state (counts, flags, etc.).
     - **Check Feasibility**: Verify if the remaining characters can be arranged to satisfy the global constraint (using precomputed properties, DP lookup, or simple conditions derived from problem structure).
     - If feasible, commit: append to result, decrement count, update state permanently.
   - Pick the smallest such candidate. If none, backtrack not needed due to greedy nature.

4. **Feasibility Check**: Critical for efficiency (must be fast, e.g., O(1) or O(26)).
   - Derive closed-form conditions (e.g., for avoiding XYZ subsequence: check if prefix enables grouping all Y before X or all Z before Y).
   - Use small state space (counts of 3 items ≤100, booleans).
   - Avoid full simulation unless n tiny.

5. **Correctness**:
   - **Lex Smallest**: Earlier positions get smallest possible without compromising feasibility.
   - **Optimality**: If a smaller prefix existed, it would have been chosen earlier.
   - **Completeness**: Feasibility ensures extension exists.

6. **Time Complexity**: O(n * 26 * check_time), where check_time << n (e.g., O(1)). For n≤100, ideal.

### When Applicable:
- Constraints local/global but verifiable quickly (subsequences of fixed length, adjacency, connectivity).
- Examples: No adjacent same, avoid short forbidden subsequence, swaps in components, monotonic stack for removals.

### Tips:
- Analyze constraint negation (e.g., avoidance conditions as OR of groupings).
- Incremental state updates prevent recomputation.
- Handle edge cases: zero counts (vacuous true), empty prefix.
- Verify with samples: simulate build path.

This approach scales to n=10^5 if check O(1), common in competitive programming.
</algorithm>
</root>
```
# Planning:
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Read number of test cases t.
2. For each test case:
   a. Read string S and string T.
   b. Let A = T[0], B = T[1], C = T[2].
   c. Compute frequency array freq[26] by counting characters in S.
   d. Initialize empty result list, progABC = 0, progAB = 0.
   e. Let n = S.length().
   f. For each position i from 0 to n-1:
      i. For each candidate ch from 'a' to 'z':
         if freq[ch - 'a'] > 0:
            // Compute new_progABC
            int new_progABC = progABC;
            if (progABC == 0 && ch == A) new_progABC = 1;
            else if (progABC == 1 && ch == B) new_progABC = 2;
            else if (progABC == 2 && ch == C) new_progABC = 3;
            // Compute new_progAB
            int new_progAB = progAB;
            if (progAB == 0 && ch == A) new_progAB = 1;
            else if (progAB == 1 && ch == B) new_progAB = 2;
            // Compute new_remC
            int new_remC = freq[C - 'a'] - (ch == C ? 1 : 0);
            // Check feasibility
            bool feasible = (new_progABC != 3) && !(new_progAB == 2 && new_remC > 0);
            if (feasible):
               result.append(ch);
               freq[ch - 'a']--;
               progABC = new_progABC;
               progAB = new_progAB;
               break;
   g. Output the string formed by joining result.
3. Repeat for all test cases.
This ensures lex smallest because we always greedily pick smallest possible character at each position such that the prefix avoids ABC subsequence so far and can be feasibly completed without ABC (no AB in prefix with remaining C>0).
Verification: Feasibility check uses automaton progress to detect ABC/AB in prefix and blocks exactly the cases where ABC is forced in prefix+suffix; greedy choice property holds as earlier positions prioritize smaller chars with extension guarantee.
<root>
<explanation>The given plan correctly solves the problem. It uses a greedy algorithm that builds the lexicographically smallest permutation by selecting the smallest available character at each position, ensuring two key conditions: (1) adding the character does not immediately complete the T subsequence (new_progABC != 3), and (2) it does not complete the AB prefix of T while leaving any C characters remaining (new_progAB != 2 or new_remC == 0). The finite automaton states (progABC and progAB) accurately track progress toward forming the forbidden T=ABC subsequence in the prefix. The feasibility check guarantees that the final string avoids ABC as a subsequence by ensuring AB is only completed after all C's are placed, preventing any A...B...C ordering. The greedy choice is safe because the constraints are local and preserve the invariant that completion is impossible without violating feasibility, and an always-extendable solution exists (e.g., placing all C's first then sorting the rest). Simulation on samples confirms it produces correct outputs like "aaaacbb", "abccc", "bcdis". No cases lead to getting stuck, as invariants prevent pathological states. The approach is efficient (O(n^2 * 26)) for constraints.</explanation>

# Planning to solve this problem.
1. For each test case:
   a. Read S and T. Let n = |S|. Compute freq[26] = counts of chars in S.
   b. Let tx = T[0]-'a', ty = T[1]-'a', tz = T[2]-'a'.

   d. For pos = 0 to n-1:
      for ch = 0 to 25:  // 'a' + ch
        if freq[ch] > 0:
          new_p = current_p
          if ch == (T[new_p] - 'a'): new_p += 1
          if new_p == 3: continue
          rx = freq[tx] - (ch == tx ? 1 : 0)
          ry = freq[ty] - (ch == ty ? 1 : 0)
          rz = freq[tz] - (ch == tz ? 1 : 0)
          if new_p != 2 || rz == 0:
            append ('a' + ch) to result
            freq[ch] -= 1
            current_p = new_p
            break
   e. Output result.

# Planning to solve this problem.
1. For each test case, read S and T. Let X = T[0], Y = T[1], Z = T[2].
2. Compute frequency count freq[26] of characters in S.
3. Initialize current_state = 0 and empty result string.
4. For each of n = |S| positions:
   a. For candidate ch from 'a' to 'z' (if freq[ch - 'a'] > 0):
      i. Temporarily decrement freq[ch - 'a'].
      ii. Compute new_state = current_state; if new_state < 3 and ch == T[new_state], new_state += 1.
      iii. Check feasibility:
         - If new_state == 3, infeasible.
         - Else if new_state == 2 and freq[Z - 'a'] > 0, infeasible.
         - Else, feasible.
      iv. If feasible, append ch to result, set current_state = new_state, break.
      v. Else, revert freq[ch - 'a'] += 1.
5. Output the result string.
This greedy builds the lexicographically smallest string position-by-position, ensuring T cannot be a subsequence via automaton state tracking and O(1) feasibility checks for remaining X/Y/Z counts.
<root>
<explanation>The given plan correctly solves the problem. It uses a greedy approach to construct the lexicographically smallest permutation of S by building it position-by-position, always choosing the smallest feasible character. The state machine (0 to 2) tracks the longest prefix of T matched so far, ensuring the automaton never reaches state 3 (which would mean T is a subsequence). The additional check prevents transitioning to state 2 if any Z remains, avoiding situations where a later Z placement would force state 3. Since X, Y, Z are distinct and the greedy leftmost matching detects subsequence existence exactly, the constructed string has no T subsequence. The approach always succeeds without backtracking, as it proactively avoids dead ends, and produces the lex-smallest valid string, as verified on samples and edge cases like S="abc". Time complexity is fine (O(26 * |S|) per test case).</explanation>

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().splitlines()
idx = 0
t = int(data[idx])
idx += 1
for _ in range(t):
    S = data[idx]
    idx += 1
    T = data[idx]
    idx += 1
    A = T[0]
    B = T[1]
    C = T[2]
    n = len(S)
    freq = [0] * 26
    for char in S:
        freq[ord(char) - ord('a')] += 1
    result = []
    progABC = 0
    progAB = 0
    for __ in range(n):
        for cand in range(26):
            ch = chr(ord('a') + cand)
            if freq[cand] > 0:
                new_progABC = progABC
                if progABC == 0 and ch == A:
                    new_progABC = 1
                elif progABC == 1 and ch == B:
                    new_progABC = 2
                elif progABC == 2 and ch == C:
                    new_progABC = 3
                new_progAB = progAB
                if progAB == 0 and ch == A:
                    new_progAB = 1
                elif progAB == 1 and ch == B:
                    new_progAB = 2
                c_idx = ord(C) - ord('a')
                new_remC = freq[c_idx] - (1 if ch == C else 0)
                feasible = (new_progABC != 3) and not (new_progAB == 2 and new_remC > 0)
                if feasible:
                    result.append(ch)
                    freq[cand] -= 1
                    progABC = new_progABC
                    progAB = new_progAB
                    break
    print(''.join(result))
```

<problem>
<description>
Given three positive integers a ≤ b ≤ c, you are provided with the sorted pairwise sums p1 = a+b ≤ p2 = a+c ≤ p3 = b+c, and the total sum s = a + b + c.

Restore a, b, c.

Input

The first line contains one integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 5000) — the number of test cases.

Each test case consists of one line with 4 integers p1 ≤ p2 ≤ p3, s (1 ≤ p1,p2,p3,s ≤ 10^9; p3 ≤ s ≤ 3*10^9).

Additional constraint: there exists at least one such a ≤ b ≤ c yielding these values.

Output

For each test case, print 3 integers a b c (a ≤ b ≤ c).
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Understand the pairwise sums: p1 = a+b, p2 = a+c, p3 = b+c (since a≤b≤c).
# Step 2: Derive formulas:
#        a = (p1 + p2 - p3) / 2  (because (a+b) + (a+c) - (b+c) = 2a)
#        b = p1 - a
#        c = p3 - b  (or equivalently s - a - b)
# Step 3: Since positive integers and guaranteed, integer division works (use //).
# Step 4: Verify a ≤ b ≤ c implicitly holds due to input constraints.
# Step 5: Use fast input for large t.
# Step 6: Output in order a b c.

import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

for _ in range(t):
    p1 = int(data[index])
    p2 = int(data[index + 1])
    p3 = int(data[index + 2])
    s = int(data[index + 3])
    index += 4
    a = (p1 + p2 - p3) // 2
    b = p1 - a
    c = s - a - b
    print(a, b, c)
</code>

6. No verification loop needed due to problem constraints.

<description>
Polycarp has an array a of 3 positive integers. He computed the prefix sums p1 = a1, p2 = a1 + a2, p3 = a1 + a2 + a3. These are given in order (strictly increasing since positives).

Restore the array a.

Input

The first line contains one integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 5000) — the number of test cases.

Each test case consists of one line which contains 3 integers p_1 < p_2 < p_3 (1 ≤ p_i ≤ 10^9; p_i < p_{i+1}).

Additional constraint: there exists at least one such array a.

Output

</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Prefix sums definition: p[0] = a[0], p[1] = a[0]+a[1], p[2] = a[0]+a[1]+a[2] (0-indexed).
# Step 2: Recover differences: a[0] = p[0], a[1] = p[1] - p[0], a[2] = p[2] - p[1].
# Step 3: Since strictly increasing and positives, all a_i >=1 automatically.
# Step 4: No sorting or assumptions needed; direct computation.
# Step 5: Fast input for multiple test cases.
# Step 6: Output the recovered array.

<algorithm>
# Identify the algorithm: Greedy
# Write a useful tutorial about the above mentioned algorithms. Provide a high level generic tutorial for solving this types of problem.

Greedy

## High-Level Generic Tutorial for Solving Reconstruction Problems from Sorted Subset Sums Using Greedy (for Small Fixed n)

### 1. **Understand the Problem Structure**
   - In problems where you reconstruct a small array (e.g., n=2 or n=3) from the sorted list of all non-empty subset sums, recognize that subset sums form a multiset with specific order properties.
   - Key insight: Singles (individual elements) dominate the smallest sums because any pair sum is at least min + second_min > second_min (since positives).

### 2. **Greedy Principle Applied**
   - **Greedily assign the smallest sums to singles**: The smallest sum b[1] (1-indexed) is always the minimum element. The second-smallest b[2] is always the second-minimum element. This holds because the smallest possible pair exceeds the second single.
   - Proof sketch: Let sorted elements be x ≤ y ≤ z. Smallest pair x+y ≥ x + y > y.
   - For larger n, extend by iteratively peeling off the smallest unassigned sums as elements, verifying against expected pairs/triples.

### 3. **Core Steps in Greedy Reconstruction**
   - **Step 1**: Extract total sum S = b[last] (full set sum).
   - **Step 2**: Greedily pick smallest k singles (for n=k), typically b[1..k] but adjust if overlaps (rare for small n).
   - **Step 3**: Compute remaining elements via S - sum_of_picked.
   - **Step 4**: Generate all subset sums from candidates, sort, and match against b (verification, O(2^n * n log n) fine for n≤20).
   - **Step 5**: If mismatch (rare due to guarantees), backtrack to alternative assignments (e.g., if b[k+1] is a small pair).

### 4. **Handling Edge Cases**
   - Duplicates: Elements equal (e.g., all equal → many repeated sums).
   - Overlaps: Pairs equaling singles (e.g., x+y = z) → appears in sorted list.
   - Large values: Use 64-bit ints; no precision issues.

### 5. **Why Greedy Works Here**
   - Optimal substructure: Smallest sums must be singles (no smaller alternatives).
   - No regret: Assigning larger to singles would contradict sorted order.
   - Time: O(1) for fixed small n; scales to O(2^n log(2^n)) with verification.

### 6. **Extensions**
   - Larger n: Combine with meet-in-middle or DP if 2^n feasible.
   - Variants: From pair sums only → algebraic greedy (smallest pair → two smallest).
   - Always verify generated sums match input for safety.

This approach shines in competitive programming for "inverse" sum problems with small n and guarantees.
</algorithm>
</root>

<description>
A string is called a palindrome if it reads the same forwards and backwards. For example, the strings "a", "aa", "aba", "abba" are palindromes. But the strings "ab", "abc", "abaa" are not.

For a given set of strings, determine if each is a palindrome.

Input

The first line contains an integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 100) — the number of test cases.

This is followed by t lines, each containing a string consisting of lowercase Latin letters with lengths between 1 and 100 inclusive.

Output

For each test case, output YES if the string is a palindrome, NO otherwise.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

# Step 1: Import necessary modules. No imports needed here.

# Step 2: Read the number of test cases.
t = int(input())

# Step 3: Loop over each test case.
for _ in range(t):
    # Step 4: Read the string and strip any whitespace.
    s = input().strip()
    
    # Step 5: Check if the string is equal to its reverse.
    # Using slicing s[::-1] to reverse the string efficiently.
    if s == s[::-1]:
        print("YES")
    else:
        print("NO")

<algorithm>
# Identify the algorithm: Brute-force.

# Write a useful tutorial about the above mentioned algorithms. Provide a high level generic tutorial for solving this types of problem.

Brute-force Algorithm Tutorial for String Repetition Checks (e.g., "Square" Strings):

1. **Understand the Problem Type**: Problems like checking if a string is "square" (exact repetition twice), or a k-th power (repeats k times), or other periodic properties, often involve verifying if the string matches a pattern derived from its prefix/suffix.

2. **Key Insight**: For "square" check:
   - Length must be even (n % 2 == 0), else impossible.
   - Let half = n // 2.
   - Verify if prefix[0:half] exactly equals suffix[half:n].

3. **High-Level Steps (Generic for Repetition Checks)**:
   - **Pre-validate**: Check length constraints (e.g., even for twice, divisible by k for k-repeats).
   - **Divide**: Compute repeat unit length (e.g., half for square).
   - **Verify Matches**: Compare segments directly (prefix vs. each subsequent block).
   - **Handle Edges**: Single char (trivial yes/no), odd lengths (no), empty (per rules).
   - **Multi-test**: Loop over t cases, read/process/output per case.

4. **Why Brute-Force?**: With n <= 100, O(n) comparison is fine (no need for KMP/Rabin-Karp unless n=10^5+). Direct slicing/matching is simplest.

5. **Generic Extensions**:
   - For k-repeats: Check n % k == 0, unit = s[0:m], then for i in range(k): if s[i*m:(i+1)*m] != unit: fail.
   - Optimizations (if needed later): Rolling hash for fast substring compare.
   - Pitfalls: Leading/trailing spaces (strip), case sensitivity (lowercase given), output casing.

6. **Time/Space**: O(t * n), space O(n) for strings.

This approach scales to small n, emphasizes clarity over premature optimization.
</algorithm>
</root>
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Read the integer t from standard input.
2. For each of the t test cases:
   a. Read the string s from standard input.
   b. Compute n = length of s.
   c. If n % 2 != 0, output "NO".
   d. Else, let half = n // 2.
   e. If s[0:half] == s[half:n], output "YES"; else, "NO".
3. Ensure output uses uppercase "YES" and "NO".
4. Time complexity: O(n) per test case where n <= 100, efficient.
5. Edge cases: n=1 (NO), n=2 ("aa" YES, "ab" NO), even lengths only, all identical chars (e.g., "aaaa" YES).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    s = data[index]
    index += 1
    n = len(s)
    if n % 2 != 0:
        print("NO")
    else:
        half = n // 2
```

# Problem 1: Graph Connectivity Check
You are given multiple test cases. For each test case, determine if an undirected graph is connected (all nodes belong to the same connected component).

Input:
The first line contains a single integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10) — the number of test cases.
For each test case:
- The first line contains two integers n m (1 ≤ n ≤ 100, 0 ≤ m ≤ 1000) — number of nodes and edges.
- Next m lines each contain two integers u v (1 ≤ u < v ≤ n) — undirected edges (no self-loops or multiple edges).

Output:
For each test case, print "YES" if the graph is connected, "NO" otherwise.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Implement Union-Find (DSU) data structure with path compression and union by rank for efficient connectivity queries.
# Step 2: Read all input at once using sys.stdin.read() for speed, as t and m are small but good practice.
# Step 3: For each test case, initialize DSU with n nodes.
# Step 4: Process all m edges by unioning the nodes.
# Step 5: After unions, find the number of unique parents (components). If exactly 1, print "YES"; else "NO".

import sys

class DSU:
    def __init__(self, n):
        self.p = list(range(n))
        self.rank = [0] * n

    def find(self, x):
        if self.p[x] != x:
            self.p[x] = self.find(self.p[x])
        return self.p[x]

    def union(self, x, y):
        px = self.find(x)
        py = self.find(y)
        if px == py:
            return False
        if self.rank[px] < self.rank[py]:
            self.p[px] = py
        elif self.rank[px] > self.rank[py]:
            self.p[py] = px
        else:
            self.p[py] = px
            self.rank[px] += 1
        return True

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    m = int(data[index + 1])
    index += 2

    dsu = DSU(n)
    for __ in range(m):
        u = int(data[index]) - 1
        v = int(data[index + 1]) - 1
        index += 2
        dsu.union(u, v)

    # Count unique parents
    components = len(set(dsu.find(i) for i in range(n)))
    if components == 1:
        print("YES")
    else:
        print("NO")
</code>
<planning>
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Recognize that connectivity requires tracking connected components efficiently → Use Union-Find (DSU).
2. Define DSU with find (path compression) and union (by rank) for amortized O(1) operations.
3. Parse input: t test cases, each with n, m, then m edges (0-index adjust).
4. For each test: init DSU(n), union all edges.
5. Compute components by set of find(i) for i in 0..n-1.
6. Check if components == 1 → YES else NO.
7. Edge cases: n=1 (always YES), m=0 (NO if n>1).
</planning>
</problem>

# Problem 2: Cycle Detection in Undirected Graph
You are given multiple test cases. For each test case, determine if an undirected graph contains a cycle.

Input:
The first line contains a single integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10) — the number of test cases.
For each test case:
- The first line contains two integers n m (1 ≤ n ≤ 100, 0 ≤ m ≤ 1000) — number of nodes and edges.
- Next m lines each contain two integers u v (1 ≤ u ≠ v ≤ n) — undirected edges (no self-loops, multiples possible but union-find handles).

Output:
For each test case, print "YES" if the graph is acyclic (a forest), "NO" if it has a cycle.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Reuse the same DSU class as before, since union returns False if already connected (indicating cycle).
# Step 2: Read input similarly.
# Step 3: For each test case, init DSU(n), process edges: if union fails (same parent), cycle detected.
# Step 4: If no cycle during all unions, "YES"; else "NO".

import sys

class DSU:
    def __init__(self, n):
        self.p = list(range(n))
        self.rank = [0] * n

    def find(self, x):
        if self.p[x] != x:
            self.p[x] = self.find(self.p[x])
        return self.p[x]

    def union(self, x, y):
        px = self.find(x)
        py = self.find(y)
        if px == py:
            return False
        if self.rank[px] < self.rank[py]:
            self.p[px] = py
        elif self.rank[px] > self.rank[py]:
            self.p[py] = px
        else:
            self.p[py] = px
            self.rank[px] += 1
        return True

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    m = int(data[index + 1])
    index += 2

    dsu = DSU(n)
    has_cycle = False
    for __ in range(m):
        u = int(data[index]) - 1
        v = int(data[index + 1]) - 1
        index += 2
        if not dsu.union(u, v):
            has_cycle = True

    if has_cycle:
        print("NO")
    else:
        print("YES")
</code>
<planning>
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Cycle exists if union attempted on already connected nodes → DSU detects via find(x)==find(y) before union.
2. Init DSU(n).
3. For each edge, attempt union; if returns False, cycle → set flag.
4. After all edges, if flag, "NO" (has cycle); else "YES" (acyclic).
5. Handles disconnected graphs (forests ok).
6. Edge cases: m=0 (YES), self-loop would be u==v but forbidden.
</planning>
</problem>

# Problem 3: Path Exists Between Two Nodes
You are given multiple test cases. For each test case, determine if there is a path between nodes s and t in an undirected graph.

Input:
The first line contains a single integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10) — the number of test cases.
For each test case:
- The first line contains four integers n m s t (1 ≤ n ≤ 100, 0 ≤ m ≤ 1000, 1 ≤ s, t ≤ n) — nodes, edges, source, target.
- Next m lines each contain two integers u v (1 ≤ u ≠ v ≤ n) — undirected edges.

Output:
For each test case, print "YES" if s and t are connected (path exists), "NO" otherwise.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Use DSU again: after unioning all edges, check if find(s) == find(t).
# Step 2: Adjust input parsing: n m s t on one "line" in data.
# Step 3: Init DSU(n), union edges, then query find(s-1) == find(t-1).

import sys

class DSU:
    def __init__(self, n):
        self.p = list(range(n))
        self.rank = [0] * n

    def find(self, x):
        if self.p[x] != x:
            self.p[x] = self.find(self.p[x])
        return self.p[x]

    def union(self, x, y):
        px = self.find(x)
        py = self.find(y)
        if px == py:
            return False
        if self.rank[px] < self.rank[py]:
            self.p[px] = py
        elif self.rank[px] > self.rank[py]:
            self.p[py] = px
        else:
            self.p[py] = px
            self.rank[px] += 1
        return True

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    m = int(data[index + 1])
    s = int(data[index + 2]) - 1
    tt = int(data[index + 3]) - 1  # t is keyword, use tt
    index += 4

    dsu = DSU(n)
    for __ in range(m):
        u = int(data[index]) - 1
        v = int(data[index + 1]) - 1
        index += 2
        dsu.union(u, v)

    if dsu.find(s) == dsu.find(tt):
        print("YES")
    else:
        print("NO")
</code>
<planning>
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Path exists iff s and t in same component → DSU find(s) == find(t).
2. Parse n m s t, note 0-index.
3. Init DSU(n), union all edges (ignores cycles).
4. Query find(s) == find(t) → YES/NO.
5. Handles s==t (YES), disconnected (NO if different comp).
</planning>
</problem>
<algorithm>
# Identify the algorithm (Brute-force, Dynamic Programming, Divide-and-conquer, Greedy, Backtracking, Recursive, Binary search, and so on) that needs to be used to solve the original problem.
Union-Find (Disjoint Set Union - DSU)

# Write a useful tutorial about the above mentioned algorithms. Provide a high level generic tutorial for solving this types of problem. Do not generate code.
Union-Find (also known as Disjoint Set Union - DSU) is a powerful data structure for efficiently managing partitions of a set into disjoint subsets, supporting two primary operations: finding the representative (root) of a set containing an element and merging (union) two sets.

High-level tutorial for solving constraint satisfaction problems like equality/inequality in graphs or cycles:

1. **Model the Problem**: Represent entities (e.g., array positions) as nodes (0 to n-1). Equality constraints ('E') imply nodes must belong to the same group (transitively). Inequality constraints ('N') require nodes to be in different groups.

2. **Initialize DSU**: Create parent array where each node is initially its own parent. Optionally, use rank array for union by rank and implement path compression in find for efficiency (achieves nearly O(1) per operation).

3. **Process Equality Constraints**: For each 'E' between neighbors i and (i+1)%n, perform union(i, (i+1)%n). This merges groups transitively, capturing chains of equalities (e.g., a1=E a2=E a3 implies a1,a2,a3 same).

4. **Verify Inequality Constraints**: After all unions, for each 'N' between i and j=(i+1)%n, check if find(i) != find(j). If find(i) == find(j), contradiction (inequality violated by transitive equality), impossible.

5. **Handle Cycles/Topology**: Works naturally for circles (use %n for wrap-around). No special cycle handling needed beyond the checks.

6. **Output**: If all 'N' checks pass, "YES" (assign same value per component, different values to different components, e.g., component id +1); else "NO".

7. **Why Efficient?**: With n≤50, trivial. Generally, α(n) ~ constant time.

8. **Generic Applications**:
   - Connectivity: Union edges, query same set.
   - Cycle detection: Union fails if already same.
   - Equality propagation with forbids: Union equals, check differs.
   - Kruskal MST: Union non-cycle edges.

This approach scales to larger n with optimizations, avoiding explicit graph BFS/DFS for components.
</algorithm>
</root>
# Planning to solve this problem.
1. Model circle positions 0 to n-1 as DSU nodes; 'E' requires union (must be equal), 'N' requires different components.
2. Define DSU with parent/rank arrays, find (path compression), union (by rank) for efficiency.
3. Parse input: t test cases, each with string s (n = s.size(), 2 ≤ n ≤ 50).
4. For each test: init DSU(n).
5. For i=0 to n-1: if s[i]=='E', union(i, (i+1)%n).
6. For i=0 to n-1: if s[i]=='N' and find(i)==find((i+1)%n), "NO".
7. If all 'N' checks pass, "YES".
8. Edge cases: n=2 "EN" (union then same root on N → NO); all 'E' (YES); all 'N' (YES); transitive E chain across N (NO).

```python
import sys

class DSU:
    def __init__(self, n):
        self.parent = list(range(n))
        self.rank = [0] * n

    def find(self, x):
        if self.parent[x] != x:
            self.parent[x] = self.find(self.parent[x])
        return self.parent[x]

    def union(self, x, y):
        px, py = self.find(x), self.find(y)
        if px == py:
            return
        if self.rank[px] < self.rank[py]:
            self.parent[px] = py
        elif self.rank[px] > self.rank[py]:
            self.parent[py] = px
        else:
            self.parent[py] = px
            self.rank[px] += 1

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    s = data[index]
    index += 1
    n = len(s)
    dsu = DSU(n)
    for i in range(n):
        if s[i] == 'E':
            dsu.union(i, (i + 1) % n)
    possible = True
    for i in range(n):
        if s[i] == 'N' and dsu.find(i) == dsu.find((i + 1) % n):
            possible = False
            break
    print("YES" if possible else "NO")
```

<description>
Problem 1: Given three positive integer lengths representing sticks, determine if it is possible to form a non-degenerate triangle using them. A triangle can be formed if the sum of any two sides is strictly greater than the third side.

Input:
- First line: integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^4) — number of test cases.
- Next t lines: three integers a, b, c (1 ≤ a, b, c ≤ 10^9) — lengths of the sticks.

Output:
- For each test case, print "YES" if a triangle can be formed, otherwise "NO".
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: Understand the triangle inequality theorem - for sides a <= b <= c, check if a + b > c (other inequalities hold automatically).
# Step 2: Use fast input reading since t=10^4 and lengths up to 10^9.
# Step 3: For each test case, sort the three lengths and apply the check.
# Step 4: Output YES or NO in uppercase.

import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    a = int(data[index])
    b = int(data[index + 1])
    c = int(data[index + 2])
    index += 3
    sides = [a, b, c]
    sides.sort()
    if sides[0] + sides[1] > sides[2]:
        print("YES")
    else:
        print("NO")
</code>

# 3. For each test case: sort the three sides into a <= b <= c.
# 4. Check if a + b > c. If yes, "YES"; else "NO".
# 5. Ensure strict inequality for non-degenerate triangle.
# 6. No edge cases beyond constraints (min 1, so no zero lengths).
</planning>

Input:
- First line: integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^4) — number of test cases.
- Next t lines: four integers a, b, c, d (1 ≤ a, b, c, d ≤ 10^9) — lengths of the sticks.

# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

input = sys.stdin.read

    a = int(data[index])

    sides = [a, b, c, d]
    sides.sort()

        print("YES")
    else:
        print("NO")

Problem 3: Given the length L of a single stick, determine if it is possible to break it into exactly three pieces of positive integer lengths that can form a non-degenerate triangle.

Input:
- First line: integer t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^4) — number of test cases.
- Next t lines: one integer L (1 ≤ L ≤ 10^8) — length of the stick.

Output:
- For each test case, print "YES" if possible, otherwise "NO".

<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.
# Step 1: To form a triangle with x + y + z = L, x,y,z >=1, need x + y > z etc. WLOG x <= y <= z < x + y = L - z => z < L/2.
# Step 2: Analysis shows impossible only for L=1,2,4. For L=3: (1,1,1); L>=5 always possible (e.g., floor(L/3) balanced).
# Step 3: Fast input for t=10^4.
# Step 4: Simple condition check.

import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    L = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    if L < 3 or L == 4:
        print("NO")
    else:
        print("YES")
</code>

<algorithm>
# Identify the algorithm: Brute-force (enumerating a small number of cases).

# Useful tutorial about Brute-force algorithm:
# Brute-force (or exhaustive search) involves systematically enumerating all possible candidates for the solution and checking each one until finding a valid one or exhausting the space. It guarantees correctness but may be inefficient for large search spaces. It's ideal for problems where the number of possibilities is small (e.g., <= 10^5 operations total).
#
# High-level generic tutorial for solving this type of problem:
# 1. Identify the decision space: Pinpoint all possible choices (e.g., which stick to break: only 3 options here).
# 2. For each choice, derive O(1) verifiable conditions (e.g., check if length == sum of others OR (even length >=2 AND others equal)).
# 3. Enumerate choices in a loop (here, just 3 if-statements).
# 4. If any choice satisfies, return YES; else NO.
# 5. Optimize by avoiding unnecessary computation (no loops over length due to 10^8 constraint; use math insights).
# 6. Applications: Small n choices (e.g., try all permutations of 5 items), small grids (3x3), or case analysis (3-10 cases).
# 7. Pros: Simple, correct. Cons: Fails if space >10^7-10^8. Improve with pruning or smarter enumeration.
# Example pattern: For "choose 1 of K items to modify, check property" — loop over K.
</algorithm>
</root>
# Planning to solve this problem.
# 1. Read all input at once using sys.stdin.read() for efficiency.
# 2. Parse t, then loop t times, reading three integers l1, l2, l3 each time.
# 3. For each test case, check three cases (brute-force over which stick to break):
#    - To break len=L keeping A,B: possible if (A == B and L % 2 == 0) or (A + B == L).
#    - Case 1: break l1 keeping l2,l3.
#    - Case 2: break l2 keeping l1,l3.
#    - Case 3: break l3 keeping l1,l2.
# 4. If any of the three cases is possible, print "YES"; else "NO".
# 5. Conditions ensure: pieces positive integers (L >= 2 implicitly satisfied when true), and four lengths form two pairs for rectangle.
# 6. No edge cases beyond constraints (min 1; L=1 can't break, conditions fail correctly; no overflow as sums <= 3*10^8).
<root>
<explanation>
The given plan correctly solves the problem. To form a rectangle with four sticks, the lengths must consist of exactly two pairs of equal lengths (a, a, b, b), possibly a square (a, a, a, a). Breaking one stick of length L into x and y (x + y = L, x, y >= 1 integers) while keeping A and B, the only ways to achieve this are:

1. Break L into A and B (requires L = A + B), resulting in A, B, A, B.
2. A == B and break L into L/2 and L/2 (requires L even, L >= 2), resulting in A, A, L/2, L/2.

No other breaks work, as other splits lead to multisets without exactly two pairs (e.g., three of one length and one different). The plan checks these exact conditions for each of the three choices of which stick to break. Constraints ensure validity: L >= 2 implicitly (conditions fail for L=1), pieces positive integers, no overflow. Input handling is efficient for t=10^4. All examples and edge cases (e.g., all 1s, odds when A=B) are handled correctly.
</explanation>

# Planning to solve this problem.
# 1. Read all input efficiently with sys.stdin.read().
# 2. Parse t, loop t times, read three integers l1, l2, l3 per test.
# 3. For each test, check three cases (break each stick):
#    def possible(break_len, a, b):
#      return (a + b == break_len) or (a == b and break_len % 2 == 0)
#    If possible(l1, l2, l3) or possible(l2, l1, l3) or possible(l3, l1, l2): "YES" else "NO".
# 4. Conditions handle L < 2 implicitly (fail both checks).

# 6. O(1) per test, handles 10^4 tests and 10^8 lengths with Python int.

The given plan correctly solves the problem. For a rectangle, the four side lengths must form two pairs of equal lengths (a, a, b, b). Breaking one stick of length `break_len` into two positive integer pieces while keeping the other two lengths `a` and `b` can achieve this in exactly two ways:

1. Break into `a` and `b` (so `a + b == break_len`), resulting in lengths a, b, a, b.
2. Break into two equal pieces (`break_len % 2 == 0`), and `a == b`, resulting in lengths `break_len//2`, `break_len//2`, a, a.

The `possible(break_len, a, b)` function checks precisely these conditions. Checking this for each of the three sticks covers all cases. No other configurations work, as verified against the problem constraints and examples:

- Handles cases where sticks cannot be broken (length < 2) implicitly.
- Works for squares (special case of rectangle).
- Efficient (O(1) per test), suitable for t=10^4 and lengths up to 10^8.
- Matches all sample inputs/outputs.

The input parsing with `sys.stdin.read()` is efficient and follows the required format.

For breaking a stick of length L into x and y (x + y = L, x, y ≥ 1), with other sticks A and B, the only configurations that work are:
- x = y = L/2 (L even) and A = B (pairs: L/2 with L/2, A with B).
- x = A and y = B (or vice versa), so L = A + B (pairs: A with A, B with B).

- Breaking l1: (l1 even and l2 == l3) or (l1 == l2 + l3).
- Similarly for l2 and l3.

```python
import sys

for _ in range(T):
    l1 = int(data[index])
    l2 = int(data[index + 1])
    l3 = int(data[index + 2])
    index += 3
    if ((l1 % 2 == 0 and l2 == l3) or l2 + l3 == l1) or \
       ((l2 % 2 == 0 and l1 == l3) or l1 + l3 == l2) or \
       ((l3 % 2 == 0 and l1 == l2) or l1 + l2 == l3):

        print("NO")
```

<problem>
<description>
# 1D Bouncing Robot Problem
A robot moves along a 1D line with positions numbered from 1 to n. It starts at position p with initial direction dr = 1 (moving right/down). Each second, before moving, if it is at a wall in the direction of movement (at 1 and dr < 0, or at n and dr > 0), it reflects by setting dr = -dr. Then it moves to position += dr. Each second (including time 0), it "cleans" its current position. Given the target position q, find the minimum time t (including 0) when it cleans q.

Input:
First line: t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^4)
Next t lines: n p q (1 ≤ n ≤ 100, 1 ≤ p, q ≤ n)

Output:
t lines, each the minimum time for that case.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

# Step 1: Understand the movement rules.
# - Start at p, dr = 1.
# - Each step: check if reflect needed: if (r == 1 and dr < 0) or (r == n and dr > 0), dr = -dr.
# - Then r += dr.
# - But at t=0, check before any move.
# - Simulate until r == q, count steps.

# Step 2: Since n <= 100, period is at most 2*(n-1) <= 198, safe to simulate without loop limit worry.

# Step 3: Read t, then for each test case:
# - If p == q, output 0.
# - Else, simulate: time = 0, r = p, dr = 1
# - Loop: time +=1, check reflect, r += dr, if r == q break.

# Step 4: Handle multiple test cases efficiently.

import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    p = int(data[index + 1])
    q = int(data[index + 2])
    index += 3
    
    if p == q:
        print(0)
        continue
    
    r = p
    dr = 1
    time = 0
    while True:
        time += 1
        if (dr > 0 and r == n) or (dr < 0 and r == 1):
            dr = -dr
        r += dr
        if r == q:
            break
    print(time)
</code>

4. Optimize input with sys.stdin.read for speed (t=1e4, n=100 -> ~1e6 ops max).
5. Handle t=0 case separately.
</planning>

<problem>
<description>
# 2D Bouncing Robot to Exact Position
A robot is in a n x m grid (rows 1..n, cols 1..m), starts at (rb, cb), dr=1, dc=1. Movement: before each move, reflect dr if (dr>0 and rb==n) or (dr<0 and rb==1); similarly for dc if (dc>0 and cb==m) or (dc<0 and cb==1). Then rb += dr, cb += dc. Find min time t >=0 to be exactly at (rd, cd).

Input:
First line: t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^4)
Next t lines: n m rb cb rd cd (1 ≤ n,m ≤ 100, 1 ≤ rb,rd ≤ n, 1 ≤ cb,cd ≤ m)

Output:
t lines, each the minimum time.
</description>
<code>
# Let's think step by step to solve this problem in Python3 programming language.

# Step 1: Similar to original but check r==rd AND c==cd.

# Step 2: Simulate both row and col independently but synced.

# Step 3: t=0 check first.
# Loop: time+=1, reflect row if needed, reflect col if needed, move both, check if both match.

# Step 4: Max steps ~ lcm(2(n-1),2(m-1)) ~ 4e4, t=1e4 -> 4e8 ops, python ok ~1s.

import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    m = int(data[index + 1])
    rb = int(data[index + 2])
    cb = int(data[index + 3])
    rd = int(data[index + 4])
    cd = int(data[index + 5])
    index += 6
    
    if rb == rd and cb == cd:
        print(0)
        continue
    
    r = rb
    c = cb
    dr = 1
    dc = 1
    time = 0
    while True:
        time += 1
        # reflect row
        if (dr > 0 and r == n) or (dr < 0 and r == 1):
            dr = -dr
        # reflect col
        if (dc > 0 and c == m) or (dc < 0 and c == 1):
            dc = -dc
        r += dr
        c += dc
        if r == rd and c == cd:
            break
    print(time)
</code>

# Bouncing Robot Path Period
A robot in n x m grid starts at (rb, cb), dr=1, dc=1. Moves with reflections as above. Find the minimal t > 0 when it returns to (rb, cb) with the SAME dr=1, dc=1 (full cycle period).

First line: t (1 ≤ t ≤ 10^4)

t lines, each the period.
</description>

# But simulate fine.

# Row cycle: time to back to rb with dr=1: 2*(n - rb +1) or something, but simulate.

<algorithm>
# Identify the algorithm: Brute-force Simulation (with independent 1D simulations for optimization).

# Tutorial on Brute-force Simulation for this type of problem:
Brute-force simulation involves directly modeling and executing the system's evolution step-by-step until the desired condition is met. For problems like bouncing robots or particles in bounded spaces with reflections:

1. **Model the state precisely**: Represent position(s) and velocity/direction components. Here, row and column are independent due to orthogonal reflections.

2. **Implement update rule accurately**: For each time step, check boundary conditions for reflection (direction towards wall from current position), reverse the relevant velocity component, then apply the (possibly new) displacement. Validate with edge cases like starting at boundary.

3. **Check condition at each step**: Including time 0. For row/column cleaning, condition is row(t) == target_row OR col(t) == target_col.

4. **Optimize where possible**: Simulate rows and columns separately to find min_t_row and min_t_col, then take min(min_t_row, min_t_col). Each 1D simulation has period O(N), so total time O(N + M) per test.

5. **Termination guarantee**: In rational-velocity billiards (here speed 1 in each axis), paths are periodic; use max_steps = 4 * max(n,m) as safeguard if needed.

6. **Efficiency for constraints**: With N,M <=100, T=1e4, operations ~1e4 * 400 = 4e6, ideal for any language.

7. **Alternatives for scale-up**: Use unfolding (straight-line in infinite grid, hit mirrored targets with parity) or compute hitting times via modular arithmetic on cycle lengths (e.g., solve for t mod 2*(n-1) where pos(t) == target).

This approach shines for small bounds, deterministic dynamics, and simple states; avoids complex math while being intuitive and debuggable.
</algorithm>
</root>

   - Else, pos = start, dir = init_dir, time = 0.

6. Handles t=0 naturally (if same row or same column initially). Guaranteed to terminate as 1D bouncing visits all positions.

1. **1D State**: pos (1..size), dir=1 initially.

## Relevant Algorithm to solve the next problem:

# Identify the algorithm: Brute-force Simulation (with independent 1D simulations for optimization).

## Tutorial on Brute-force Simulation for this type of problem:
Brute-force simulation involves directly modeling and executing the system's evolution step-by-step until the desired condition is met. For problems like bouncing robots or particles in bounded spaces with reflections:

1. **Model the state precisely**: Position and velocity/direction independent per dimension (row/column) due to orthogonal reflections.

2. **Implement update rule accurately**: Tentative reflection if at boundary and velocity points outward: if (pos==1 and v<0) or (pos==N and v>0), reverse v=-v, then pos += v. Handles discrete grid correctly (verified for n,m>=1; t=0 check avoids edge simulation issues).

3. **Check condition at each step**: For cleaning, first check t=0 (same row OR column). Then min(t_row where row(t)==rd, t_col where col(t)==cd).

4. **Optimize where possible**: Simulate 1D rows/columns separately for t_row/t_col, then min(t_row, t_col). Each 1D has O(N) steps max, total O(n+m) per test.

5. **Termination guarantee**: 1D paths periodic with period <=2*N; hits every position. Use max_steps=4*max(n,m) safeguard.

6. **Efficiency for constraints**: N,M<=100, T=1e4, ops ~1e4*400=4e6, ideal.

7. **Alternatives for scale-up**: Unfolding (t mod 2N with parity for reflections) or LCM of row/col periods, but simulation suffices.

This approach shines for small bounds, deterministic dynamics, simple states; intuitive, debuggable, no math errors.
<root>
<explanation>The given plan correctly solves the problem. The robot cleans the dirty cell at time t if its row or column matches the dirty cell's row or column at that time. The initial check for t=0 (same row or column) is accurate. For t >= 1, row and column movements are independent due to orthogonal reflections, so simulating 1D row and column paths separately to find the minimal t_row and t_col where the position matches rd or cd, then taking the minimum, precisely identifies the earliest time. The 1D simulation accurately implements the pre-movement reflection rule: check boundaries based on current position and velocity, reverse velocity if needed, then update position, and check post-update. Constraints ensure efficiency (O(n + m) per test, fine for t=1e4, n,m<=100). Edge cases like n=1 or m=1 are handled by the t=0 check, avoiding simulation issues. The approach matches the problem's dynamics and examples, with guaranteed termination as 1D paths visit all positions.</explanation>
<confidence>100</confidence>

```python
import sys

def simulate(size, start, target, init_dir):
    if start == target:
        return 0
    pos = start
    dirr = init_dir
    time = 0
    while True:
        time += 1
        if pos == 1 and dirr == -1:
            dirr = -dirr
        elif pos == size and dirr == 1:
            dirr = -dirr
        pos += dirr
        if pos == target:
            return time

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    m = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    r_b = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    c_b = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    r_d = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    c_d = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    
    t_row = simulate(n, r_b, r_d, 1)
    t_col = simulate(m, c_b, c_d, 1)
    print(min(t_row, t_col))
```
